<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Calcium</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">

				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the metallic element. <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_calcium" title="Template:Infobox calcium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_calcium" title="Template talk:Infobox calcium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_calcium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Calcium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Ca</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 20. An <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth metal</a>, calcium is a reactive metal that forms a dark oxide-nitride layer when exposed to air. Its physical and chemical properties are most similar to its heavier homologues <a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">strontium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">barium</a>. It is the fifth most abundant element in Earth's crust and the third most abundant metal, after <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>. The most common calcium compound on Earth is <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>, found in <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> and the fossilised remnants of early sea life; <a href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anhydrite" title="Anhydrite">anhydrite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fluorite" title="Fluorite">fluorite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Apatite" title="Apatite">apatite</a> are also sources of calcium. The name derives from <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <i>calx</i> "lime", which was obtained from heating limestone.
</p><p>Some calcium compounds were known to the ancients, though their chemistry was unknown until the seventeenth century. Pure calcium was isolated in 1808 via <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of its oxide by <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a>, who named the element. Calcium compounds are widely used in many industries: in foods and pharmaceuticals for calcium supplementation, in the paper industry as bleaches, as components in cement and electrical insulators, and in the manufacture of soaps. On the other hand, the metal in pure form has few applications due to its high reactivity; still, in small quantities it is often used as an alloying component in steelmaking, and sometimes, as a calcium–lead alloy, in making automotive batteries.
</p><p>Calcium is the most abundant metal and the fifth-most abundant element in the <a href="/wiki/Human_body#Composition" title="Human body">human body</a>. As <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolytes</a>, calcium ions play a vital role in the <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemical</a> processes of organisms and <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>: in <a href="/wiki/Signal_transduction" title="Signal transduction">signal transduction</a> pathways where they act as a <a href="/wiki/Second_messenger" class="mw-redirect" title="Second messenger">second messenger</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> release from <a href="/wiki/Neurons" class="mw-redirect" title="Neurons">neurons</a>; in contraction of all <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a> cell types; as cofactors in many <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>; and in <a href="/wiki/Fertilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertilization">fertilization</a>. Calcium ions outside cells are important for maintaining the <a href="/wiki/Potential_difference" class="mw-redirect" title="Potential difference">potential difference</a> across excitable <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membranes</a> as well as proper bone formation.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h3>
<p>Calcium is a very ductile silvery metal (sometimes described as pale yellow) whose properties are very similar to the heavier elements in its group, <a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">strontium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">barium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>. A calcium atom has twenty electrons, arranged in the <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configuration</a> [Ar]4s<sup>2</sup>. Like the other elements placed in group 2 of the periodic table, calcium has two <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a> in the outermost s-orbital, which are very easily lost in chemical reactions to form a dipositive ion with the stable electron configuration of a <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a>, in this case <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>. Hence, calcium is almost always <a href="/wiki/Divalent" class="mw-redirect" title="Divalent">divalent</a> in its compounds, which are usually <a href="/wiki/Ionic_compound" title="Ionic compound">ionic</a>. Hypothetical univalent salts of calcium would be stable with respect to their elements, but not to <a href="/wiki/Disproportionation" title="Disproportionation">disproportionation</a> to the divalent salts and calcium metal, because the <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy_of_formation" class="mw-redirect" title="Enthalpy of formation">enthalpy of formation</a> of MX<sub>2</sub> is much higher than those of the hypothetical MX. This occurs because of the much greater <a href="/wiki/Lattice_energy" title="Lattice energy">lattice energy</a> afforded by the more highly charged Ca<sup>2+</sup> cation compared to the hypothetical Ca<sup>+</sup> cation.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood112_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood112-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Calcium, strontium, barium, and radium are always considered to be <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth metals</a>; the lighter <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, also in group 2 of the periodic table, are often included as well. Nevertheless, beryllium and magnesium are significantly different from the other members of the group in their physical and chemical behaviour: they behave more like <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> respectively and have some of the weaker metallic character of the <a href="/wiki/Post-transition_metal" title="Post-transition metal">post-transition metals</a>, which is why the traditional definition of the term "alkaline earth metal" excludes them.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This classification is mostly obsolete in English-language sources, but is still used in other countries such as Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> As a result, comparisons with strontium and barium are more germane to calcium chemistry than comparisons with magnesium.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood112_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood112-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span></h3>
<p>Calcium metal melts at 842&#160;°C and boils at 1494&#160;°C; these values are higher than those for magnesium and strontium, the neighbouring group 2 metals. It crystallises in the <a href="/wiki/Face-centered_cubic" class="mw-redirect" title="Face-centered cubic">face-centered cubic</a> arrangement like strontium; above 450&#160;°C, it changes to an <a href="/wiki/Anisotropy" title="Anisotropy">anisotropic</a> <a href="/wiki/Hexagonal_close-packed" class="mw-redirect" title="Hexagonal close-packed">hexagonal close-packed</a> arrangement like magnesium. Its density of 1.55&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup> is the lowest in its group.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood112_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood112-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Calcium is harder than <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> but can be cut with a knife with effort. While calcium is a poorer conductor of electricity than <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> by volume, it is a better conductor by mass than both due to its very low density.<sup id="cite_ref-Ropp_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ropp-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> While calcium is infeasible as a conductor for most terrestrial applications as it reacts quickly with atmospheric oxygen, its use as such in space has been considered.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann484_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann484-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">Chemical</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ca(aq)6_improved_image.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ca%28aq%296_improved_image.tif/lossless-page1-220px-Ca%28aq%296_improved_image.tif.png" width="220" height="104" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ca%28aq%296_improved_image.tif/lossless-page1-330px-Ca%28aq%296_improved_image.tif.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ca%28aq%296_improved_image.tif/lossless-page1-440px-Ca%28aq%296_improved_image.tif.png 2x" data-file-width="665" data-file-height="315" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ca(aq)6_improved_image.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of the polymeric [Ca(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>2+</sup> center in hydrated calcium chloride, illustrating the high coordination number typical for calcium complexes.</div></div></div>
<p>The chemistry of calcium is that of a typical heavy alkaline earth metal. For example, calcium spontaneously reacts with water more quickly than magnesium and less quickly than strontium to produce <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydroxide" title="Calcium hydroxide">calcium hydroxide</a> and hydrogen gas. It also reacts with the <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> in the air to form a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">calcium oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_nitride" title="Calcium nitride">calcium nitride</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> When finely divided, it spontaneously burns in air to produce the nitride. In bulk, calcium is less reactive: it quickly forms a hydration coating in moist air, but below 30% <a href="/wiki/Relative_humidity" title="Relative humidity">relative humidity</a> it may be stored indefinitely at room temperature.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann483_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann483-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides the simple oxide CaO, the <a href="/wiki/Peroxide" title="Peroxide">peroxide</a> CaO<sub>2</sub> can be made by direct oxidation of calcium metal under a high pressure of oxygen, and there is some evidence for a yellow <a href="/wiki/Superoxide" title="Superoxide">superoxide</a> Ca(O<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Calcium hydroxide, Ca(OH)<sub>2</sub>, is a strong base, though it is not as strong as the hydroxides of strontium, barium or the alkali metals.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> All four dihalides of calcium are known.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">Calcium carbonate</a> (CaCO<sub>3</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_sulfate" title="Calcium sulfate">calcium sulfate</a> (CaSO<sub>4</sub>) are particularly abundant minerals.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood122_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood122-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Like strontium and barium, as well as the alkali metals and the divalent <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a> <a href="/wiki/Europium" title="Europium">europium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ytterbium" title="Ytterbium">ytterbium</a>, calcium metal dissolves directly in liquid <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> to give a dark blue solution.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood112_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood112-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the large size of the Ca<sup>2+</sup> ion, high coordination numbers are common, up to 24 in some <a href="/wiki/Intermetallic_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Intermetallic compound">intermetallic compounds</a> such as CaZn<sub>13</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Calcium is readily complexed by oxygen <a href="/wiki/Chelate" class="mw-redirect" title="Chelate">chelates</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid" title="Ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid">EDTA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polyphosphate" title="Polyphosphate">polyphosphates</a>, which are useful in <a href="/wiki/Analytic_chemistry" class="mw-redirect" title="Analytic chemistry">analytic chemistry</a> and removing calcium ions from <a href="/wiki/Hard_water" title="Hard water">hard water</a>. In the absence of <a href="/wiki/Steric_hindrance" class="mw-redirect" title="Steric hindrance">steric hindrance</a>, smaller group 2 cations tend to form stronger complexes, but when large <a href="/wiki/Polydentate" class="mw-redirect" title="Polydentate">polydentate</a> <a href="/wiki/Macrocycle" title="Macrocycle">macrocycles</a> are involved the trend is reversed.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood122_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood122-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although calcium is in the same group as magnesium and <a href="/wiki/Organomagnesium_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Organomagnesium compound">organomagnesium compounds</a> are very commonly used throughout chemistry, organocalcium compounds are not similarly widespread because they are more difficult to make and more reactive, although they have recently been investigated as possible <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalysts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Organocalcium compounds tend to be more similar to organoytterbium compounds due to the similar <a href="/wiki/Ionic_radius" title="Ionic radius">ionic radii</a> of Yb<sup>2+</sup> (102&#160;pm) and Ca<sup>2+</sup> (100&#160;pm). Most of these compounds can only be prepared at low temperatures; bulky ligands tend to favor stability. For example, calcium di<a href="/wiki/Cyclopentadienyl" title="Cyclopentadienyl">cyclopentadienyl</a>, Ca(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, must be made by directly reacting calcium metal with <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mercurocene&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mercurocene (page does not exist)">mercurocene</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentadiene" title="Cyclopentadiene">cyclopentadiene</a> itself; replacing the C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub> ligand with the bulkier C<sub>5</sub>(CH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>5</sub> ligand on the other hand increases the compound's solubility, volatility, and kinetic stability.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_calcium" title="Isotopes of calcium">Isotopes of calcium</a></div>
<p>Natural calcium is a mixture of five stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> (<sup>40</sup>Ca, <sup>42</sup>Ca, <sup>43</sup>Ca, <sup>44</sup>Ca, and <sup>46</sup>Ca) and one isotope with a half-life so long that it can be considered stable for all practical purposes (<a href="/wiki/Calcium-48" title="Calcium-48"><sup>48</sup>Ca</a>, with a half-life of about 4.3&#160;×&#160;10<sup>19</sup>&#160;years). Calcium is the first (lightest) element to have six naturally occurring isotopes.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By far the most common isotope of calcium in nature is <sup>40</sup>Ca, which makes up 96.941% of all natural calcium. It is produced in the <a href="/wiki/Silicon-burning_process" title="Silicon-burning process">silicon-burning process</a> from fusion of <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a> and is the heaviest stable nuclide with equal proton and neutron numbers; its occurrence is also supplemented slowly by the decay of <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial</a> <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40"><sup>40</sup>K</a>. Adding another alpha particle would lead to unstable <sup>44</sup>Ti, which quickly decays via two successive <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron captures</a> to stable <sup>44</sup>Ca; this makes up 2.806% of all natural calcium and is the second-most common isotope. The other four natural isotopes, <sup>42</sup>Ca, <sup>43</sup>Ca, <sup>46</sup>Ca, and <sup>48</sup>Ca, are significantly rarer, each comprising less than 1% of all natural calcium. The four lighter isotopes are mainly products of the <a href="/wiki/Oxygen-burning_process" title="Oxygen-burning process">oxygen-burning</a> and silicon-burning processes, leaving the two heavier ones to be produced via neutron-capturing processes. <sup>46</sup>Ca is mostly produced in a "hot" <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a>, as its formation requires a rather high neutron flux to allow short-lived <sup>45</sup>Ca to capture a neutron. <sup>48</sup>Ca is produced by <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> in the <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">r-process</a> in <a href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernova" title="Type Ia supernova">type Ia supernovae</a>, where high neutron excess and low enough entropy ensures its survival.<sup id="cite_ref-Cameron_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clayton_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><sup>46</sup>Ca and <sup>48</sup>Ca are the first "classically stable" nuclides with a six-neutron or eight-neutron excess respectively. Although extremely neutron-rich for such a light element, <sup>48</sup>Ca is very stable because it is a <a href="/wiki/Magic_number_(physics)" title="Magic number (physics)">doubly magic nucleus</a>, having 20 protons and 28 neutrons arranged in closed shells. Its <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> to <sup>48</sup><a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">Sc</a> is very hindered because of the gross mismatch of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_spin" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear spin">nuclear spin</a>: <sup>48</sup>Ca has zero nuclear spin, being <a href="/wiki/Even_and_odd_atomic_nuclei" title="Even and odd atomic nuclei">even–even</a>, while <sup>48</sup>Sc has spin 6+, so the decay is <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_mechanism" title="Forbidden mechanism">forbidden</a> by the conservation of <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>. While two excited states of <sup>48</sup>Sc are available for decay as well, they are also forbidden due to their high spins. As a result, when <sup>48</sup>Ca does decay, it does so by <a href="/wiki/Double_beta_decay" title="Double beta decay">double beta decay</a> to <sup>48</sup><a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">Ti</a> instead, being the lightest nuclide known to undergo double beta decay.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The heavy isotope <sup>46</sup>Ca can also theoretically undergo double beta decay to <sup>46</sup>Ti as well, but this has never been observed; the lightest and most common isotope <sup>40</sup>Ca is also doubly magic and could undergo double <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> to <sup>40</sup><a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">Ar</a>, but this has likewise never been observed. Calcium is the only element to have two primordial doubly magic isotopes. The experimental lower limits for the half-lives of <sup>40</sup>Ca and <sup>46</sup>Ca are 5.9&#160;×&#160;10<sup>21</sup>&#160;years and 2.8&#160;×&#160;10<sup>15</sup>&#160;years respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Apart from the practically stable <sup>48</sup>Ca, the longest lived <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope">radioisotope</a> of calcium is <sup>41</sup>Ca. It decays by electron capture to stable <sup>41</sup><a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">K</a> with a half-life of about a hundred thousand years. Its existence in the early Solar System as an <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">extinct radionuclide</a> has been inferred from excesses of <sup>41</sup>K: traces of <sup>41</sup>Ca also still exist today, as it is a <a href="/wiki/Cosmogenic_nuclide" title="Cosmogenic nuclide">cosmogenic nuclide</a>, continuously reformed through <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">neutron activation</a> of natural <sup>40</sup>Ca.<sup id="cite_ref-Clayton_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Many other calcium radioisotopes are known, ranging from <sup>34</sup>Ca to <sup>57</sup>Ca: they are all much shorter-lived than <sup>41</sup>Ca, the most stable among them being <sup>45</sup>Ca (half-life 163&#160;days) and <sup>47</sup>Ca (half-life 4.54&#160;days). The isotopes lighter than <sup>42</sup>Ca usually undergo <a href="/wiki/Beta_plus_decay" class="mw-redirect" title="Beta plus decay">beta plus decay</a> to isotopes of potassium, and those heavier than <sup>44</sup>Ca usually undergo <a href="/wiki/Beta_minus_decay" class="mw-redirect" title="Beta minus decay">beta minus decay</a> to isotopes of <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a>, although near the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_drip_line" title="Nuclear drip line">nuclear drip lines</a> <a href="/wiki/Proton_emission" title="Proton emission">proton emission</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron_emission" title="Neutron emission">neutron emission</a> begin to be significant decay modes as well.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Like other elements, a variety of processes alter the relative abundance of calcium isotopes.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The best studied of these processes is the mass-dependent <a href="/wiki/Isotope_fractionation" title="Isotope fractionation">fractionation</a> of calcium isotopes that accompanies the precipitation of calcium minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aragonite" title="Aragonite">aragonite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apatite" title="Apatite">apatite</a> from solution. Lighter isotopes are preferentially incorporated into these minerals, leaving the surrounding solution enriched in heavier isotopes at a magnitude of roughly 0.025% per atomic mass unit (amu) at room temperature. Mass-dependent differences in calcium isotope composition are conventionally expressed by the ratio of two isotopes (usually <sup>44</sup>Ca/<sup>40</sup>Ca) in a sample compared to the same ratio in a standard reference material. <sup>44</sup>Ca/<sup>40</sup>Ca varies by about 1% among common earth materials.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:92px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ein_Ghazal_Venus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Ein_Ghazal_Venus.jpg/90px-Ein_Ghazal_Venus.jpg" width="90" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Ein_Ghazal_Venus.jpg/135px-Ein_Ghazal_Venus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Ein_Ghazal_Venus.jpg/180px-Ein_Ghazal_Venus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="196" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ein_Ghazal_Venus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the <a href="/wiki/%27Ain_Ghazal_Statues" title="&#39;Ain Ghazal Statues">'Ain Ghazal Statues</a>, made from <a href="/wiki/Lime_plaster" title="Lime plaster">lime plaster</a></div></div></div>
<p>Calcium compounds were known for millennia, although their chemical makeup was not understood until the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood108_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood108-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Lime as a <a href="/wiki/Lime_(material)" title="Lime (material)">building material</a><sup id="cite_ref-minerals.usgs_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-minerals.usgs-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and as <a href="/wiki/Lime_plaster" title="Lime plaster">plaster for statues</a> was used as far back as around 7000&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The first dated <a href="/wiki/Lime_kiln" title="Lime kiln">lime kiln</a> dates back to 2500&#160;BC and was found in <a href="/wiki/Khafajah" title="Khafajah">Khafajah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> At about the same time, dehydrated <a href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a> (CaSO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O) was being used in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Great Pyramid of Giza</a>; this material would later be used for the plaster in the tomb of <a href="/wiki/Tutankhamun" title="Tutankhamun">Tutankhamun</a>. The climate of present-day Italy being warmer than that of Egypt, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Roman">ancient Romans</a> instead used lime mortars made by heating <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> (CaCO<sub>3</sub>); the name "calcium" itself derives from the Latin word <i>calx</i> "lime".<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood108_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood108-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a> noted that the lime that resulted was lighter than the original limestone, attributing this to the boiling of the water; in 1755, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Black" title="Joseph Black">Joseph Black</a> proved that this was due to the loss of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, which as a gas had not been recognised by the ancient Romans.<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
In 1787, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> suspected that lime might be an oxide of a fundamental <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a>. In his table of the elements, Lavoisier listed five "salifiable earths" (i.e., ores that could be made to react with acids to produce salts (<i>salis</i> = salt, in Latin): <i>chaux</i> (calcium oxide), <i>magnésie</i> (magnesia, magnesium oxide), <i>baryte</i> (barium sulfate), <i>alumine</i> (alumina, aluminium oxide), and <i>silice</i> (silica, silicon dioxide). About these "elements", Lavoisier speculated: </p><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We are probably only acquainted as yet with a part of the metallic substances existing in nature, as all those which have a stronger affinity to oxygen than carbon possesses, are incapable, hitherto, of being reduced to a metallic state, and consequently, being only presented to our observation under the form of oxyds, are confounded with earths. It is extremely probable that barytes, which we have just now arranged with earths, is in this situation; for in many experiments it exhibits properties nearly approaching to those of metallic bodies. It is even possible that all the substances we call earths may be only metallic oxyds, irreducible by any hitherto known process.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Calcium, along with its congeners magnesium, strontium, and barium, was first isolated by <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> in 1808. Following the work of <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jakob_Berzelius" class="mw-redirect" title="Jöns Jakob Berzelius">Jöns Jakob Berzelius</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnus_Martin_af_Pontin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Magnus Martin af Pontin (page does not exist)">Magnus Martin af Pontin</a> on <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a>, Davy isolated calcium and magnesium by putting a mixture of the respective metal oxides with <a href="/wiki/Mercury(II)_oxide" title="Mercury(II) oxide">mercury(II) oxide</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> plate which was used as the anode, the cathode being a platinum wire partially submerged into mercury. Electrolysis then gave calcium–mercury and magnesium–mercury amalgams, and distilling off the mercury gave the metal.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood108_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood108-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> However, pure calcium cannot be prepared in bulk by this method and a workable commercial process for its production was not found until over a century later.<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_production">Occurrence and production</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pamukkale_Hierapolis_Travertine_pools.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Pamukkale_Hierapolis_Travertine_pools.JPG/220px-Pamukkale_Hierapolis_Travertine_pools.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Pamukkale_Hierapolis_Travertine_pools.JPG/330px-Pamukkale_Hierapolis_Travertine_pools.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Pamukkale_Hierapolis_Travertine_pools.JPG/440px-Pamukkale_Hierapolis_Travertine_pools.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="2136" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pamukkale_Hierapolis_Travertine_pools.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Travertine" title="Travertine">Travertine</a> terraces in <a href="/wiki/Pamukkale" title="Pamukkale">Pamukkale</a>, Turkey</div></div></div>
<p>At 3%, calcium is the fifth <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">most abundant element in the Earth's crust</a>, and the third most abundant metal behind <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood108_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood108-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> It is also the fourth most abundant element in the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_highlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Lunar highlands">lunar highlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann483_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann483-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rocks" class="mw-redirect" title="Sedimentary rocks">Sedimentary</a> <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> deposits pervade the Earth's surface as fossilized remains of past marine life; they occur in two forms, the <a href="/wiki/Rhombohedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhombohedral">rhombohedral</a> <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a> (more common) and the <a href="/wiki/Orthorhombic" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthorhombic">orthorhombic</a> <a href="/wiki/Aragonite" title="Aragonite">aragonite</a> (forming in more temperate seas). Minerals of the first type include <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chalk" title="Chalk">chalk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iceland_spar" title="Iceland spar">iceland spar</a>; aragonite beds make up the <a href="/wiki/Bahamas" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahamas">Bahamas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Keys" title="Florida Keys">Florida Keys</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> basins. <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">Corals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_shell" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea shell">sea shells</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pearl" title="Pearl">pearls</a> are mostly made up of calcium carbonate. Among the other important minerals of calcium are <a href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a> (CaSO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O), <a href="/wiki/Anhydrite" title="Anhydrite">anhydrite</a> (CaSO<sub>4</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Fluorite" title="Fluorite">fluorite</a> (CaF<sub>2</sub>), and <a href="/wiki/Apatite" title="Apatite">apatite</a> ([Ca<sub>5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>F]).<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood108_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood108-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The major producers of calcium are <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (about 10000 to 12000 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> per year), <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> (about 6000 to 8000 tonnes per year), and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (about 2000 to 4000 tonnes per year). <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> are also among the minor producers. In 2005, about 24000 tonnes of calcium were produced; about half of the world's extracted calcium is used by the United States, with about 80% of the output used each year.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann484_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann484-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In Russia and China, Davy's method of electrolysis is still used, but is instead applied to molten <a href="/wiki/Calcium_chloride" title="Calcium chloride">calcium chloride</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann484_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann484-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Since calcium is less reactive than strontium or barium, the oxide–nitride coating that results in air is stable and <a href="/wiki/Lathe" title="Lathe">lathe</a> machining and other standard metallurgical techniques are suitable for calcium.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood110_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood110-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In the United States and Canada, calcium is instead produced by reducing lime with aluminium at high temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann484_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann484-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geochemical_cycling">Geochemical cycling</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbonate%E2%80%93silicate_cycle" title="Carbonate–silicate cycle">Carbonate–silicate cycle</a></div>
<p>Calcium provides a link between <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">tectonics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>. In the simplest terms, uplift of mountains exposes calcium-bearing rocks to chemical weathering and releases Ca<sup>2+</sup> into surface water. These ions are transported to the ocean where they react with dissolved CO<sub>2</sub> to form <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CaCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), which in turn settles to the sea floor where it is incorporated into new rocks. Dissolved CO<sub>2</sub>, along with <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a> ions, are termed "<a href="/wiki/Total_inorganic_carbon" title="Total inorganic carbon">dissolved inorganic carbon</a>" (DIC).<sup id="cite_ref-Berner_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berner-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The actual reaction is more complicated and involves the bicarbonate ion (HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>) that forms when CO<sub>2</sub> reacts with water at seawater <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Ca<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + 2<span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">CaCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></a></span><sub>(<a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">s</a>)</sub> + <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></dd></dl>
<p>At seawater pH, most of the CO<sub>2</sub> is immediately converted back into <span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>. The reaction results in a net transport of one molecule of CO<sub>2</sub> from the ocean/atmosphere into the <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The result is that each Ca<sup>2+</sup> ion released by chemical weathering ultimately removes one CO<sub>2</sub> molecule from the surficial system (atmosphere, ocean, soils and living organisms), storing it in carbonate rocks where it is likely to stay for hundreds of millions of years. The weathering of calcium from rocks thus scrubs CO<sub>2</sub> from the ocean and atmosphere, exerting a strong long-term effect on climate.<sup id="cite_ref-Berner_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berner-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>
<p>The largest use of calcium is in <a href="/wiki/Steelmaking" title="Steelmaking">steelmaking</a>, due to its strong <a href="/wiki/Chemical_affinity" title="Chemical affinity">chemical affinity</a> for oxygen and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>. Its oxides and sulfides, once formed, give liquid lime <a href="/wiki/Aluminate" title="Aluminate">aluminate</a> and sulfide inclusions in steel which float out; on treatment, these inclusions disperse throughout the steel and became small and spherical, improving castability, cleanliness and general mechanical properties. Calcium is also used in maintenance-free <a href="/wiki/Automotive_battery" title="Automotive battery">automotive batteries</a>, in which the use of 0.1% calcium–<a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> alloys instead of the usual <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>–lead alloys leads to lower water loss and lower self-discharging. Due to the risk of expansion and cracking, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> is sometimes also incorporated into these alloys. These lead–calcium alloys are also used in casting, replacing lead–antimony alloys.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann485_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann485-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Calcium is also used to strengthen aluminium alloys used for bearings, for the control of graphitic <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a>, and to remove <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a> impurities from lead.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood110_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood110-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Calcium metal is found in some drain cleaners, where it functions to generate heat and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydroxide" title="Calcium hydroxide">calcium hydroxide</a> that <a href="/wiki/Saponification" title="Saponification">saponifies</a> the fats and liquefies the proteins (for example, those in hair) that block drains.<sup id="cite_ref-Rumack_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rumack-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Besides metallurgy, the reactivity of calcium is exploited to remove <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> from high-purity <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> gas and as a <a href="/wiki/Getter" title="Getter">getter</a> for oxygen and nitrogen. It is also used as a reducing agent in the production of <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>. It can also be used to store hydrogen gas, as it reacts with hydrogen to form solid <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydride" title="Calcium hydride">calcium hydride</a>, from which the hydrogen can easily be re-extracted.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood110_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood110-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Calcium isotope fractionation during mineral formation has led to several applications of calcium isotopes. In particular, the 1997 observation by Skulan and DePaolo<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> that calcium minerals are isotopically lighter than the solutions from which the minerals precipitate is the basis of analogous applications in medicine and in paleooceanography. In animals with skeletons mineralized with calcium, the calcium isotopic composition of soft tissues reflects the relative rate of formation and dissolution of skeletal mineral. In humans, changes in the calcium isotopic composition of urine have been shown to be related to changes in bone mineral balance. When the rate of bone formation exceeds the rate of bone resorption, the <sup>44</sup>Ca/<sup>40</sup>Ca ratio in soft tissue rises and vice versa. Because of this relationship, calcium isotopic measurements of urine or blood may be useful in the early detection of metabolic bone diseases like <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">osteoporosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> A similar system exists in seawater, where <sup>44</sup>Ca/<sup>40</sup>Ca tends to rise when the rate of removal of Ca<sup>2+</sup> by mineral precipitation exceeds the input of new calcium into the ocean. In 1997 Skulan and DePaolo presented the first evidence of change in seawater <sup>44</sup>Ca/<sup>40</sup>Ca over geologic time, along with a theoretical explanation of these changes. More recent papers have confirmed this observation, demonstrating that seawater Ca<sup>2+</sup> concentration is not constant, and that the ocean is never in a "steady state" with respect to calcium input and output. This has important climatological implications, as the marine calcium cycle is closely tied to the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many calcium compounds are used in food, as pharmaceuticals, and in medicine, among others. For example, calcium and phosphorus are supplemented in foods through the addition of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_lactate" title="Calcium lactate">calcium lactate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium_diphosphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Calcium diphosphate">calcium diphosphate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tricalcium_phosphate" title="Tricalcium phosphate">tricalcium phosphate</a>. The last is also used as a polishing agent in <a href="/wiki/Toothpaste" title="Toothpaste">toothpaste</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Antacid" title="Antacid">antacids</a>. <a href="/wiki/Calcium_lactobionate" class="mw-redirect" title="Calcium lactobionate">Calcium lactobionate</a> is a white powder that is used as a suspending agent for pharmaceuticals. In baking, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Calcium_monophosphate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Calcium monophosphate (page does not exist)">calcium monophosphate</a> is used as a <a href="/wiki/Leavening_agent" title="Leavening agent">leavening agent</a>. <a href="/wiki/Calcium_sulfite" title="Calcium sulfite">Calcium sulfite</a> is used as a bleach in papermaking and as a disinfectant, <a href="/wiki/Calcium_silicate" title="Calcium silicate">calcium silicate</a> is used as a reinforcing agent in rubber, and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_acetate" title="Calcium acetate">calcium acetate</a> is a component of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Liming_rosin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Liming rosin (page does not exist)">liming rosin</a> and is used to make metallic soaps and synthetic resins.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann485_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann485-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_and_pathological_role">Biological and pathological role</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Calcium_in_biology" title="Calcium in biology">Calcium in biology</a></div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Calcium_intake_world_map.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Calcium_intake_world_map.svg/310px-Calcium_intake_world_map.svg.png" width="310" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Calcium_intake_world_map.svg/465px-Calcium_intake_world_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Calcium_intake_world_map.svg/620px-Calcium_intake_world_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1314" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Calcium_intake_world_map.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global dietary calcium intake among adults (mg/day).<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r853264625">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{list-style-type:none;margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>dl>dd{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em;list-style:none}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-100{font-size:100%}</style><div class="refbegin columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;"> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #aa2b2c; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;400-500</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fa7929; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;500-600</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #a46e2c; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;600-700</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fee940; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;700-800</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #829d29; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;800-900</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #84fc5a; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;900-1000</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #2d621d; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;&gt;1000</div> </div></div></div></div>
<p>Calcium is an <a href="/wiki/Essential_element" class="mw-redirect" title="Essential element">essential element</a> needed in large quantities. The Ca<sup>2+</sup> ion acts as an <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolyte</a> and is vital to the health of the muscular, circulatory, and digestive systems; is indispensable to the building of bone; and supports synthesis and function of blood cells. For example, it regulates the contraction of muscles, nerve conduction, and the clotting of blood. As a result, intra- and extracellular calcium levels are tightly regulated by the body. Calcium can play this role because the Ca<sup>2+</sup> ion forms stable <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complexes</a> with many organic compounds, especially <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>; it also forms compounds with a wide range of solubilities, enabling the formation of <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeletons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann489_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann489-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Calcium ions may be complexed by proteins through binding the <a href="/wiki/Carboxyl_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Carboxyl group">carboxyl groups</a> of <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">glutamic acid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aspartic_acid" title="Aspartic acid">aspartic acid</a> residues; through interacting with <a href="/wiki/Phosphorylation" title="Phosphorylation">phosphorylated</a> <a href="/wiki/Serine" title="Serine">serine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyrosine" title="Tyrosine">tyrosine</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Threonine" title="Threonine">threonine</a> residues; or by being <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelated</a> by γ-carboxylated amino acid residues. <a href="/wiki/Trypsin" title="Trypsin">Trypsin</a>, a digestive enzyme, uses the first method; <a href="/wiki/Osteocalcin" title="Osteocalcin">osteocalcin</a>, a bone matrix protein, uses the third. Some other bone matrix proteins such as <a href="/wiki/Osteopontin" title="Osteopontin">osteopontin</a> and bone <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sialoprotein&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sialoprotein (page does not exist)">sialoprotein</a> use both the first and the second. Direct activation of enzymes by binding calcium is common; some other enzymes are activated by noncovalent association with direct calcium-binding enzymes. Calcium also binds to the <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipid</a> layer of the <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a>, anchoring proteins associated with the cell surface.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann489_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann489-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> As an example of the wide range of solubility of calcium compounds, <a href="/wiki/Monocalcium_phosphate" title="Monocalcium phosphate">monocalcium phosphate</a> is very soluble in water, 85% of extracellular calcium is as <a href="/wiki/Dicalcium_phosphate" title="Dicalcium phosphate">dicalcium phosphate</a> with a solubility of 2.0&#160;<a href="/wiki/Molar_concentration" title="Molar concentration">mM</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyapatite" title="Hydroxyapatite">hydroxyapatite</a> of bones in an organic matrix is <a href="/wiki/Tricalcium_phosphate" title="Tricalcium phosphate">tricalcium phosphate</a> at 100&#160;µM.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann489_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann489-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>About three-quarters of dietary calcium is from dairy products and grains, the rest being accounted for by vegetables, protein-rich foods, fruits, sugar, fats, and oil. Calcium supplementation is controversial, as the bioavailability of calcium is strongly dependent on the solubility of the salt involved: calcium citrate, malate, and lactate are highly bioavailable while the oxalate is much less so. The intestine absorbs about one-third of calcium eaten as the <a href="/wiki/Radical_ion" title="Radical ion">free ion</a>, and plasma calcium level is then regulated by the kidneys. <a href="/wiki/Parathyroid_hormone" title="Parathyroid hormone">Parathyroid hormone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">vitamin D</a> promote the formation of bone by allowing and enhancing the deposition of calcium ions there, allowing rapid bone turnover without affecting bone mass or mineral content. When plasma calcium levels fall, cell surface receptors are activated and the secretion of parathyroid hormone occurs; it then proceeds to stimulate the entry of calcium into the plasma pool by taking it from targeted kidney, gut, and bone cells, with the bone-forming action of parathyroid hormone being antagonised by <a href="/wiki/Calcitonin" title="Calcitonin">calcitonin</a>, whose secretion increases with increasing plasma calcium levels.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann489_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann489-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Excess intake of calcium may cause <a href="/wiki/Hypercalcaemia" title="Hypercalcaemia">hypercalcaemia</a>, but because of the inefficient absorption of calcium by the intestines a more likely cause is excessive vitamin D intake or excessive secretion of parathyroid hormone. It can also occur due to the bone destruction that occurs when tumours metastasise to bone. This results in deposition of calcium salts into the heart, the blood vessels, and the kidneys. Symptoms include anorexia, nausea, vomiting, memory loss, confusion, muscle weakness, increased urination, dehydration, and metabolic bone disease. Chronic hypercalcaemia may lead to soft tissue <a href="/wiki/Calcification" title="Calcification">calcification</a>, which can lead to serious consequences: for example, calcification of the <a href="/wiki/Vascular_wall" class="mw-redirect" title="Vascular wall">vascular wall</a> can lead to a loss of elasticity and the disruption of laminar blood flow, and thence to <a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_plaque" title="Vulnerable plaque">plaque rupture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thrombosis" title="Thrombosis">thrombosis</a>. Likewise, inadequate calcium or vitamin D intake results in <a href="/wiki/Hypocalcaemia" title="Hypocalcaemia">hypocalcaemia</a>, often caused by inadequate secretion of parathyroid hormone or defective receptors to it in cells. Symptoms include neuromuscular excitability, potentially causing <a href="/wiki/Tetany" title="Tetany">tetany</a> and defects in cardiac conduction.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann489_49-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann489-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As calcium is heavily involved in bone manufacture, many bone diseases can be traced to problems with the organic matrix or the hydroxyapatite in molecular structure or organisation. For example, <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">osteoporosis</a> is a reduction in mineral content of bone per unit volume, and can be treated by supplementation of calcium, vitamin D, and biphosphates. Calcium supplements may benefit the serum lipids in women who have passed <a href="/wiki/Menopause" title="Menopause">menopause</a> as well as older men; in post-menopausal women calcium supplementation also appears to be inversely correlated with cardiovascular disease. Inadequate amounts of calcium, vitamin D, or phosphates can lead to the softening of bones, known as <a href="/wiki/Osteomalacia" title="Osteomalacia">osteomalacia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann489_49-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann489-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

E.g., calcium" title="Reactivity code 1: Normally stable, but can become unstable at elevated temperatures and pressures. E.g., calcium">1</span></a></div><div style="vertical-align:middle; text-align:center; line-height:80%; position:absolute; top:52px;"><a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#White" title="NFPA 704"><span style="font-size:15px; color:black; width:23px; position:absolute; left:29px;" title="Special hazard W: Reacts with water in an unusual or dangerous manner. E.g., cesium, sodium"><s>W</s></span></a></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>




</tbody></table>
<p>Because calcium reacts exothermically with water and acids, calcium metal coming into contact with bodily moisture results in severe corrosive irritation.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann487_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann487-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> When swallowed, calcium metal has the same effect on the mouth, oesophagus, and stomach, and can be fatal.<sup id="cite_ref-Rumack_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rumack-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> However, long-term exposure is not known to have distinct adverse effects.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann487_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann487-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calcium_in_food">Calcium in food</span></h3>
<p>Because of concerns of long-term adverse side effects such as calcification of arteries and kidney stones, the U.S. Institute of Medicine (IOM) and the <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> (EFSA) both set <a href="/wiki/Tolerable_upper_intake_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Tolerable upper intake level">Tolerable Upper Intake Levels</a> (ULs) for the combination of dietary and supplemental calcium. From the IOM, people ages 9–18 years are not supposed to exceed 3,000&#160;mg/day; for ages 19–50 not to exceed 2,500&#160;mg/day; for ages 51 and older, not to exceed 2,000&#160;mg/day.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The EFSA set UL at 2,500&#160;mg/day for adults but decided the information for children and adolescents was not sufficient to determine ULs.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
